<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Universe</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
								<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Universe_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Universe (disambiguation)">Universe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<caption>Universe</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg" class="image"><img alt="NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/300px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg" width="300" height="274" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/450px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/600px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2300" data-file-height="2100" /></a>
<div>The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">Hubble Ultra-Deep Field</a> image shows some of the most remote <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> visible with present technology, each consisting of billions of stars. (Apparent image area about 1/79 that of a full moon)<sup id="cite_ref-spacetelescope.org_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacetelescope.org-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">Age</a> (within <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>)</th>
<td>13.799 ± 0.021 billion years<sup id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Diameter</th>
<td>At least 156–554&#160;billion light-years (47.8–170&#160;billion parsecs)<sup id="cite_ref-msu_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msu-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extra-Dimensions_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extra-Dimensions-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-spacedotcom156_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacedotcom156-5">[5]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap">(maybe infinite<sup id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-6">[6]</a></sup>)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Mass (ordinary matter)</th>
<td>At least 10<sup>53</sup> kg<sup id="cite_ref-Paul_Davies_2006_43_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Davies_2006_43-7">[7]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Average density</th>
<td>4.5 x 10<sup>-31</sup> g/cm<sup>3</sup><sup id="cite_ref-wmap_universe_made_of_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wmap_universe_made_of-8">[8]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Average temperature</th>
<td>2.72548 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a><sup id="cite_ref-Fixsen_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fixsen-9">[9]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Main Contents</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">Ordinary (baryonic)</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> (4.9%)<br />
<a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a> (26.8%)<br />
<a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a> (68.3%)<sup id="cite_ref-planck2013parameters_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-planck2013parameters-10">[10]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Shape</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">Flat</a> with only a 0.4% margin of error<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Universe</b> is all of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a><sup id="cite_ref-spacetime_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacetime-12">[a]</a></sup> and their contents,<sup id="cite_ref-Zeilik1998_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeilik1998-13">[12]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, and all other forms of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. While the spatial size of the entire Universe is still unknown,<sup id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-6">[6]</a></sup> it is possible to measure the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>.</p>
<p>The earliest scientific models of the Universe were developed by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophers</a> and were <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric</a>, placing <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> at the centre of the Universe.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-15">[14]</a></sup> Over the centuries, more precise astronomical observations led <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> to develop the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric model</a> with the Sun at the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. In developing the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">law of universal gravitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a> built upon Copernicus's work as well as observations by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">laws of planetary motion</a>.</p>
<p>Further observational improvements led to the realization that our Solar System is located in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy, which is one of many galaxies in the Universe. It is assumed that galaxies are distributed uniformly and the same in all directions, meaning that the Universe has neither an edge nor a center. Discoveries in the early 20th century have suggested that the Universe had a beginning and that it is expanding<sup id="cite_ref-Hawking_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking-16">[15]</a></sup> at an increasing rate.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_2011_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_2011-17">[16]</a></sup> Roughly eighty percent of mass in the Universe appears to exist in an unknown form called <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> which cannot be directly observed.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory is the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmological</a> description of the development of the Universe. Under this theory, space and time emerged together <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7001137990000000000?</span>13.799<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.021&#160;billion years</span> ago<sup id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-2">[2]</a></sup> with a fixed amount of energy and matter that has become less dense as the Universe has expanded. After the initial expansion, the Universe cooled, allowing the first <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a> to form and then simple <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>. Giant clouds later merged through gravity to form galaxies, stars, and everything else seen today. It is possible to see objects that are now further away than 13.799 billion <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> because <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">space itself has expanded</a>. This means that objects which are now 46 billion light years away can <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">still be seen</a> in their distant past, because at that time they were much closer to us.</p>
<p>There are many competing hypotheses about the <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">ultimate fate of the universe</a> and about what, if anything, preceded the Big Bang, while other physicists and philosophers refuse to speculate, doubting that information about prior states will ever be accessible. Some physicists have suggested various <a href="/wiki/Multiverse" title="Multiverse">multiverse</a> hypotheses, in which the Universe might be one among many universes that likewise exist.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EllisKS032_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EllisKS032-19">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<p>The physical Universe is defined as all of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a><sup id="cite_ref-spacetime_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacetime-12">[a]</a></sup> (collectively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>) and their contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Zeilik1998_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeilik1998-13">[12]</a></sup> Such contents encompass all of energy in its various forms, including <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>, and therefore planets, <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, stars, galaxies, and the contents of <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">intergalactic space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-21">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> The Universe also includes the <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a> that influence energy and matter, such as <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law" title="Conservation law">conservation laws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schreuder2014_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schreuder2014-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Universe is often defined as "the totality of existence", or <a href="/wiki/Everything" title="Everything">everything</a> that exists, everything that has existed, and everything that will exist.<sup id="cite_ref-Schreuder2014_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schreuder2014-24">[23]</a></sup> In fact, some philosophers and scientists support the inclusion of ideas and abstract concepts – such as mathematics and logic – in the definition of the Universe.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> The word <i>universe</i> may also refer to concepts such as <i>the cosmos</i>, <i>the world</i>, and <i>nature</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bolonkin2011_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolonkin2011-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>universe</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> word <i>univers</i>, which in turn derives from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>universum</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The Latin word was used by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and later Latin authors in many of the same senses as the modern <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word is used.<sup id="cite_ref-lewis_short_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lewis_short-31">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synonyms">Synonyms</span></h3>
<p>A term for "universe" among the ancient Greek philosophers from <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> onwards was <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text" xml:lang="grc">t? p??</span>, <i>tò pân</i> ("the all"), defined as all matter and all space, and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text" xml:lang="grc">t? ????</span>, <i>tò hólon</i> ("all things"), which did not necessarily include the void.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Another synonym was <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text" xml:lang="grc">? ??sµ??</span>, <i>ho kósmos</i> (meaning the <a href="/wiki/World_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="World (philosophy)">world</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Synonyms are also found in Latin authors (<i>totum</i>, <i>mundus</i>, <i>natura</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> and survive in modern languages, e.g., the German words <i>Das All</i>, <i>Weltall</i>, and <i>Natur</i> for <i>Universe</i>. The same synonyms are found in English, such as everything (as in the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a>), the cosmos (as in <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>), the world (as in the <a href="/wiki/Many-worlds_interpretation" title="Many-worlds interpretation">many-worlds interpretation</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> (as in <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural laws</a> or <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology_and_the_Big_Bang">Chronology and the Big Bang</span></h2>

<div id="Legend" class="toccolours" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">billion years</a><br />
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<p>The prevailing model for the evolution of the Universe is the Big Bang theory.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> The Big Bang model states that the earliest state of the Universe was an extremely hot and dense one, and that the Universe subsequently expanded and cooled. The model is based on <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> and on simplifying assumptions such as <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)#Translation_invariance" title="Homogeneity (physics)">homogeneity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropy</a> of space. A version of the model with a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> (Lambda) and <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>, is the simplest model that provides a reasonably good account of various observations about the Universe. The Big Bang model accounts for observations such as the correlation of distance and <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of galaxies, the ratio of the number of hydrogen to helium atoms, and the microwave radiation background.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/300px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" width="300" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/450px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/600px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3078" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>In this diagram, time passes from left to right, so at any given time, the Universe is represented by a disk-shaped "slice" of the diagram.</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The initial hot, dense state is called the <a href="/wiki/Planck_epoch" class="mw-redirect" title="Planck epoch">Planck epoch</a>, a brief period extending from time zero to one <a href="/wiki/Planck_time" title="Planck time">Planck time</a> unit of approximately 10<sup>-43</sup> seconds. During the Planck epoch, all types of matter and all types of energy were concentrated into a dense state, where <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> is believed to have been as strong as the other <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental force">fundamental forces</a>, and all the forces may have been <a href="/wiki/Grand_unification" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand unification">unified</a>. Since the Planck epoch, the Universe has been <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">expanding</a> to its present form, possibly with a very brief period of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a> which caused the Universe to reach a much larger size in less than <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Notation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific Notation">10<sup>-32</sup></a> seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-Sivaram_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sivaram-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Planck epoch and inflation came the <a href="/wiki/Quark_epoch" title="Quark epoch">quark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadron_epoch" title="Hadron epoch">hadron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lepton_epoch" title="Lepton epoch">lepton epochs</a>. Together, these epochs encompassed less than 10 seconds of time following the Big Bang. The observed abundance of the elements can be explained by combining the overall expansion of space with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_physics" title="Atomic physics">atomic physics</a>. As the Universe expands, the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> decreases more quickly than does that of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> because the energy of a photon decreases with its wavelength. As the Universe expanded and cooled, <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a> associated stably into ever larger combinations. Thus, in the early part of the matter-dominated era, stable <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> formed, which then formed <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a> through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactions" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear reactions">nuclear reactions</a>. This process, known as <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>, led to the present abundances of lighter nuclei, particularly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Big Bang nucleosynthesis ended about 20 minutes after the Big Bang, when the Universe had cooled enough so that <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> could no longer occur. At this stage, matter in the Universe was mainly a hot, dense <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> of negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, neutral <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> and positive nuclei. This era, called the <a href="/wiki/Photon_epoch" title="Photon epoch">photon epoch</a>, lasted about 380 thousand years.</p>
<p>Eventually, at a time known as <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a>, electrons and nuclei formed stable atoms, which are transparent to most wavelengths of radiation. With photons decoupled from matter, the Universe entered the <a href="/wiki/Matter-dominated_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Matter-dominated era">matter-dominated era</a>. Light from this era could travel freely, and it can still be seen in the Universe as the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (CMB). After around 100 million years, the first <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> formed; these were likely very massive, luminous, and responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a> of the Universe. Having no <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">elements heavier than lithium</a>, these stars also produced the first heavy elements through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The Universe also contains a mysterious energy called <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, the density of which does not change over time. After about 9.8 billion years, the Universe had expanded sufficiently so that the density of matter was less than the density of dark energy, marking the beginning of the present <a href="/wiki/Dark-energy-dominated_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark-energy-dominated era">dark-energy-dominated era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> In this era, the expansion of the Universe is <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">accelerating</a> due to dark energy.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">Observable universe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of the Universe">Age of the Universe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">Metric expansion of space</a></div>
<p>Of the four <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> is the dominant at astronomical length scales. Gravity's effects are cumulative; by contrast, the effects of positive and negative charges tend to cancel one another, making electromagnetism relatively insignificant on astronomical length scales. The remaining two interactions, the <a href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak nuclear force">weak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Strong nuclear force">strong nuclear forces</a>, decline very rapidly with distance; their effects are confined mainly to sub-atomic length scales.</p>
<p>The Universe appears to have much more <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> than <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, an asymmetry possibly related to the <a href="/wiki/CP_violation" title="CP violation">CP violation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> This imbalance between matter and antimatter is partially responsible for the existence of the Universe in itself, since matter and antimatter, if equally produced at the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, would have completely annihilated each other and, as a result, the universe would not have existed.<sup id="cite_ref-TT-20171025_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TT-20171025-43">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NAT-20171020_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20171020-44">[43]</a></sup> The Universe also appears to have neither net <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, which follows accepted physical laws if the Universe is finite. These laws are the <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law" title="Gauss's law">Gauss's law</a> and the non-divergence of the <a href="/wiki/Stress-energy-momentum_pseudotensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress-energy-momentum pseudotensor">stress-energy-momentum pseudotensor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0.5em auto; width:800px;">
<tr>
<th>Constituent spatial scales of the observable universe</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="font-size:88%"><a href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_(JPEG).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Earth's Location in the Universe SMALLER (JPEG).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg/800px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg" width="800" height="400" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg/1200px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg/1600px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_%28JPEG%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5000" data-file-height="2500" /></a>

</td>

</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_regions">Size and regions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">Observable universe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Observational_cosmology" title="Observational cosmology">Observational cosmology</a></div>
<p>The size of the Universe is somewhat difficult to define. According to the general theory of relativity, some regions of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> may never interact with ours even in the lifetime of the Universe due to the finite <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> and the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Expansion of space">expansion of space</a>. For example, radio messages sent from Earth may never reach some regions of space, even if the Universe were to exist forever: space may expand faster than light can traverse it.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaku2008_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaku2008-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Distant regions of space are assumed to exist and to be part of reality as much as we are, even though we can never interact with them. The spatial region that we can affect and be affected by is the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The observable universe depends on the location of the observer. By traveling, an observer can come into contact with a greater region of spacetime than an observer who remains still. Nevertheless, even the most rapid traveler will not be able to interact with all of space. Typically, the observable universe is taken to mean the portion of the Universe that is observable from our vantage point in the Milky Way.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" class="mw-redirect" title="Comoving distance">proper distance</a>—the distance as would be measured at a specific time, including the present—between <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and the edge of the observable universe is 46&#160;billion light-years (14&#160;billion parsecs), making the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Size" title="Observable universe">diameter of the observable universe</a> about 91&#160;billion light-years (28<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;pc). The distance the light from the edge of the observable universe has travelled is very close to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of the Universe">age of the Universe</a> times the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, 13.8&#160;billion light-years (4.2<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;pc), but this does not represent the distance at any given time because the edge of the observable universe and the Earth have since moved further apart.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> For comparison, the diameter of a typical <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> is 30,000 light-years (9,198 <a href="/wiki/Parsecs" class="mw-redirect" title="Parsecs">parsecs</a>), and the typical distance between two neighboring galaxies is 3 million <a href="/wiki/Light-years" class="mw-redirect" title="Light-years">light-years</a> (919.8 kiloparsecs).<sup id="cite_ref-r196_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r196-48">[47]</a></sup> As an example, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> is roughly 100,000–180,000 light years in diameter,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> and the nearest sister galaxy to the Milky Way, the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, is located roughly 2.5 million light years away.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because we cannot observe space beyond the edge of the observable universe, it is unknown whether the size of the Universe in its totality is finite or infinite.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Estimates for the total size of the universe, if finite, reach as high as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 10^{10^{10^{122}}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msup>
          <mn>10</mn>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <msup>
              <mn>10</mn>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <msup>
                  <mn>10</mn>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mn>122</mn>
                  </mrow>
                </msup>
              </mrow>

          </mrow>
        </msup>

    </mrow>
